			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Helen Keller</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>

<tr>

<p><b>Helen Adams Keller</b> (June 27, 1880 – June 1, 1968) was an American author, political activist, and lecturer. She was the first <a href="/wiki/Deaf-blindness" class="mw-redirect" title="Deaf-blindness">deaf-blind</a> person to earn a bachelor of arts degree. The story of how Keller's teacher, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Sullivan" title="Anne Sullivan">Anne Sullivan</a>, broke through the isolation imposed by a near complete lack of language, allowing the girl to blossom as she learned to communicate, has become widely known through the dramatic depictions of the play and film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_Worker" title="The Miracle Worker">The Miracle Worker</a></i>. Her birthplace in West <a href="/wiki/Tuscumbia,_Alabama" title="Tuscumbia, Alabama">Tuscumbia, Alabama</a>, is now a museum<sup id="cite_ref-birthplace_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-1">[1]</a></sup> and sponsors an annual "Helen Keller Day". Her birthday on June 27 is commemorated as Helen Keller Day in the U.S. state of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and was authorized at the federal level by presidential proclamation by President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> in 1980, the 100th anniversary of her birth.</p>
<p>A prolific author, Keller was well-traveled and outspoken in her convictions. A member of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialist Party of America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Workers_of_the_World" title="Industrial Workers of the World">Industrial Workers of the World</a>, she campaigned for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labor_rights" title="Labor rights">labor rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimilitarism" title="Antimilitarism">antimilitarism</a>, and other similar causes. She was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Women%27s_Hall_of_Fame" title="Alabama Women's Hall of Fame">Alabama Women's Hall of Fame</a> in 1971<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and was one of twelve inaugural inductees to the Alabama Writers Hall of Fame on June 8, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Keller proved to the world that deaf people could all learn to communicate and that they could survive in the hearing world. She also taught that deaf people are capable of doing things that hearing people can do. One of the most famous deaf people in history, she is an idol to many deaf people in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_childhood_and_illness">Early childhood and illness</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg/220px-Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg/330px-Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg/440px-Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="633" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_Keller_with_Anne_Sullivan_in_July_1888.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Keller with <a href="/wiki/Anne_Sullivan_Macy" class="mw-redirect" title="Anne Sullivan Macy">Anne Sullivan</a> vacationing on <a href="/wiki/Cape_Cod" title="Cape Cod">Cape Cod</a> in July 1888</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Helen Adams Keller was born on June 27, 1880, in <a href="/wiki/Tuscumbia,_Alabama" title="Tuscumbia, Alabama">Tuscumbia, Alabama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-perkinsFAQ_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perkinsFAQ-5">[5]</a></sup> Her family lived on a homestead, <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Green" title="Ivy Green">Ivy Green</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-birthplace_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-1">[1]</a></sup> that Helen's grandfather had built decades earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen2007_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen2007-6">[6]</a></sup> She had two siblings, Mildred Campbell and Phillip Brooks Keller, and two older half-brothers from her father's prior marriage, James and William Simpson Keller.<sup id="cite_ref-Ask1006_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ask1006-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her father, Arthur H. Keller,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> spent many years as an editor for the Tuscumbia <i>North Alabamian</i>, and had served as a captain for the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Confederate Army">Confederate Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-perkinsFAQ_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perkinsFAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen2007_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen2007-6">[6]</a></sup> Her paternal grandmother was second cousins with <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StoryofmyLife_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StoryofmyLife-10">[10]</a></sup> Her mother, Kate Adams,<sup id="cite_ref-Kate_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kate-11">[11]</a></sup> was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Adams_(Confederate_general)" title="Charles W. Adams (Confederate general)">Charles W. Adams</a>, a Confederate general.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Though originally from Massachusetts, Charles Adams also fought for the Confederate Army during the American Civil War, earning the rank of colonel (and acting brigadier-general). Her paternal lineage was traced to Casper Keller, a native of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StoryofmyLife_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StoryofmyLife-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ask1105_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ask1105-13">[13]</a></sup> One of Helen's Swiss ancestors was the first teacher for the deaf in Zurich. Keller reflected on this coincidence in her first autobiography, stating "that there is no king who has not had a slave among his ancestors, and no slave who has not had a king among his."<sup id="cite_ref-StoryofmyLife_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StoryofmyLife-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Helen Keller was born with the ability to see and hear. At 19 months old, she contracted an unknown illness described by doctors as "an acute congestion of the stomach and the brain",<sup id="cite_ref-askKellerIllness_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-askKellerIllness-14">[14]</a></sup> which might have been <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_fever" title="Scarlet fever">scarlet fever</a> or <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-perkinsFAQ_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perkinsFAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The illness left her both deaf and blind. At that time, she was able to communicate somewhat with Martha Washington, the six-year-old daughter of the family cook, who understood her signs;<sup id="cite_ref-mylife_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mylife-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup> by the age of seven, Keller had more than 60 <a href="/wiki/Home_sign" title="Home sign">home signs</a> to communicate with her family. Even though blind and deaf, Helen Keller had passed through many obstacles and she learned to live with her disabilities. She learned how to tell which person was walking by from the vibrations their footsteps would make. The sex and age of the person could be identified by how strong and continuous the steps were.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1886, Keller's mother, inspired by an account in <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/American_Notes" title="American Notes">American Notes</a></i> of the successful education of another deaf and blind woman, <a href="/wiki/Laura_Bridgman" title="Laura Bridgman">Laura Bridgman</a>, dispatched the young Keller, accompanied by her father, to seek out physician J. Julian Chisolm, an eye, ear, nose, and throat specialist in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, for advice.<sup id="cite_ref-Worthington_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worthington-18">[18]</a></sup> Chisholm referred the Kellers to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a>, who was working with deaf children at the time. Bell advised them to contact the <a href="/wiki/Perkins_Institute_for_the_Blind" class="mw-redirect" title="Perkins Institute for the Blind">Perkins Institute for the Blind</a>, the school where Bridgman had been educated, which was then located in <a href="/wiki/South_Boston,_Boston,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="South Boston, Boston, Massachusetts">South Boston</a>. Michael Anagnos, the school's director, asked 20-year-old former student <a href="/wiki/Anne_Sullivan_Macy" class="mw-redirect" title="Anne Sullivan Macy">Anne Sullivan</a>, herself visually impaired, to become Keller's instructor. It was the beginning of a 49-year-long relationship during which Sullivan evolved into Keller's <a href="/wiki/Governess" title="Governess">governess</a> and eventually her <a href="/wiki/Lady%27s_companion" title="Lady's companion">companion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mylife_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mylife-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:Introduction, Key Figures</sup></p>
<p>Sullivan arrived at Keller's house in March 1887, and immediately began to teach Helen to communicate by spelling words into her hand, beginning with "d-o-l-l" for the doll that she had brought Keller as a present. Keller was frustrated, at first, because she did not understand that every object had a word uniquely identifying it. In fact, when Sullivan was trying to teach Keller the word for "mug", Keller became so frustrated she broke the mug.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkie_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkie-19">[19]</a></sup> Keller's breakthrough in communication came the next month, when she realized that the motions her teacher was making on the palm of her hand, while running cool water over her other hand, symbolized the idea of "water"; she then nearly exhausted Sullivan demanding the names of all the other familiar objects in her world.</p>
<p>Helen Keller was viewed as isolated, but was very in touch with the outside world. She was able to enjoy music by feeling the beat and she was able to have a strong connection with animals through touch. She was delayed at picking up language, but that did not stop her from having a voice.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_education">Formal education</span></h2>
<p>In May 1888, Keller started attending the Perkins Institute for the Blind. In 1894, Keller and Sullivan moved to New York to attend the <a href="/wiki/Wright-Humason_School_for_the_Deaf" title="Wright-Humason School for the Deaf">Wright-Humason School for the Deaf</a>, and to learn from <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Fuller" title="Sarah Fuller">Sarah Fuller</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Horace_Mann_School_for_the_Deaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Horace Mann School for the Deaf">Horace Mann School for the Deaf</a>. In 1896, they returned to Massachusetts, and Keller entered <a href="/wiki/The_Cambridge_School_of_Weston" title="The Cambridge School of Weston">The Cambridge School for Young Ladies</a> before gaining admittance, in 1900, to <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-radcliff_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radcliff-21">[21]</a></sup> where she lived in Briggs Hall, <a href="/wiki/Cabot_House" title="Cabot House">South House</a>. Her admirer, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>, had introduced her to <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a> magnate <a href="/wiki/Henry_H._Rogers" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry H. Rogers">Henry Huttleston Rogers</a>, who, with his wife Abbie, paid for her education. In 1904, at the age of 24, Keller graduated from Radcliffe, becoming the first deaf blind person to earn a Bachelor of Arts degree. She maintained a correspondence with the Austrian philosopher and <a href="/wiki/Pedagogue" class="mw-redirect" title="Pedagogue">pedagogue</a> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Jerusalem" title="Wilhelm Jerusalem">Wilhelm Jerusalem</a>, who was one of the first to discover her literary talent.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Determined to communicate with others as conventionally as possible, Keller learned to speak, and spent much of her life giving speeches and lectures on aspects of her life. She learned to "hear" people's speech by reading their lips with her hands—her sense of touch had heightened. She became proficient at using <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">braille</a><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and reading <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a> with her hands as well. Shortly before World War I, with the assistance of the <a href="/wiki/Zoellner_Quartet" title="Zoellner Quartet">Zoellner Quartet</a>, she determined that by placing her fingertips on a resonant tabletop she could experience music played close by.<sup id="cite_ref-Spectrum2_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spectrum2-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_of_her_lectures">Example of her lectures</span></h2>
<p>On January 22, 1916, Keller and Sullivan traveled to the small town of <a href="/wiki/Menomonie,_Wisconsin" title="Menomonie, Wisconsin">Menomonie</a> in western Wisconsin to deliver a lecture at the <a href="/wiki/Mabel_Tainter_Memorial_Building" title="Mabel Tainter Memorial Building">Mabel Tainter Memorial Building</a>. Details of her talk were provided in the weekly <i>Dunn County News</i> on January 22, 1916:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>A message of optimism, of hope, of good cheer, and of loving service was brought to Menomonie Saturday—a message that will linger long with those fortunate enough to have received it. This message came with the visit of Helen Keller and her teacher, Mrs. John Macy, and both had a hand in imparting it Saturday evening to a splendid audience that filled The Memorial. The wonderful girl who has so brilliantly triumphed over the triple afflictions of blindness, dumbness and deafness, gave a talk with her own lips on "Happiness," and it will be remembered always as a piece of inspired teaching by those who heard it.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>When part of the account was reprinted in the January 20, 2016, edition of the paper under the heading "From the Files", the column compiler added</p>
<blockquote>
<p>According to those who attended, Helen Keller spoke of the joy that life gave her. She was thankful for the faculties and abilities that she did possess and stated that the most productive pleasures she had were curiosity and imagination. Keller also spoke of the joy of service and the happiness that came from doing things for others&#160;... Keller imparted that "helping your fellow men were one's only excuse for being in this world and in the doing of things to help one's fellows lay the secret of lasting happiness." She also told of the joys of loving work and accomplishment and the happiness of achievement. Although the entire lecture lasted only a little over an hour, the lecture had a profound impact on the audience.<sup id="cite_ref-Koser_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koser-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Companions">Companions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png/220px-PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png/330px-PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png/440px-PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png 2x" data-file-width="1492" data-file-height="1997" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PSM_V63_D081_Helen_keller_and_miss_sullivan.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Helen Keller in 1899 with lifelong companion and teacher Anne Sullivan. Photo taken by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a> at his School of Vocal Physiology and Mechanics of Speech.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anne_Sullivan" title="Anne Sullivan">Anne Sullivan</a> stayed as a companion to Helen Keller long after she taught her. Sullivan married John Macy in 1905, and her health started failing around 1914. Polly Thomson (1855 – March 21, 1960) was hired to keep house. She was a young woman from Scotland who had no experience with deaf or blind people. She progressed to working as a secretary as well, and eventually became a constant companion to Keller.<sup id="cite_ref-grace_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grace-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Keller moved to <a href="/wiki/Forest_Hills,_Queens" title="Forest Hills, Queens">Forest Hills, Queens</a>, together with Sullivan and Macy, and used the house as a base for her efforts on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/American_Foundation_for_the_Blind" title="American Foundation for the Blind">American Foundation for the Blind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rnib_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnib-27">[27]</a></sup> "While in her thirties Helen had a love affair, became secretly engaged, and defied her teacher and family by attempting an elopement with the man she loved."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He was "Peter Fagan, a young <a href="/wiki/Boston_Herald" title="Boston Herald">Boston Herald</a> reporter who was sent to Helen's home to act as her private secretary when lifelong companion, Anne, fell ill."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Anne Sullivan died in 1936 after a coma as a result of coronary thrombosis,<sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:266</sup> with Keller holding her hand.<sup id="cite_ref-Herrmann_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrmann-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:255</sup> Keller and Thomson moved to <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>. They traveled worldwide and raised funds for the blind. Thomson had a stroke in 1957 from which she never fully recovered, and died in 1960. Winnie Corbally, a nurse whom they originally hired to care for Thomson in 1957, stayed on after her death and was Keller's companion for the rest of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-rnib_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnib-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_activities">Political activities</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_KellerA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Helen_KellerA.jpg/220px-Helen_KellerA.jpg" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Helen_KellerA.jpg/330px-Helen_KellerA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Helen_KellerA.jpg/440px-Helen_KellerA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2837" data-file-height="3534" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_KellerA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Helen Keller portrait, 1904. Due to a protruding left eye, Keller was usually photographed in profile. Both her eyes were replaced in adulthood with <a href="/wiki/Ocular_prosthesis" title="Ocular prosthesis">glass replicas</a> for "medical and cosmetic reasons".<sup id="cite_ref-Herrmann_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrmann-31">[31]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>"The few own the many because they possess the means of livelihood of all&#160;... The country is governed for the richest, for the corporations, the bankers, the land speculators, and for the exploiters of labor. The majority of mankind are working people. So long as their fair demands—the ownership and control of their livelihoods—are set at naught, we can have neither men's rights nor women's rights. The majority of mankind is ground down by industrial oppression in order that the small remnant may live in ease."</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Helen Keller, 1911<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Keller went on to become a world-famous speaker and author. She is remembered as an <a href="/wiki/List_of_disability_rights_activists" title="List of disability rights activists">advocate for people with disabilities</a>, amid numerous other causes. The Deaf community was widely impacted by her. She traveled to twenty-five different countries giving motivational speeches about Deaf people's conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> She was a <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">suffragette</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacifist" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacifist">pacifist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radical_politics" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical politics">radical</a> socialist, <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> supporter, and opponent of <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>. In 1915 she and George A. Kessler founded the <a href="/wiki/Helen_Keller_International" title="Helen Keller International">Helen Keller International</a> (HKI) organization. This organization is devoted to research in vision, health and nutrition. In 1920, she helped to found the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_Liberties_Union" title="American Civil Liberties Union">American Civil Liberties Union</a> (ACLU). Keller traveled to over 40 countries with Sullivan, making several trips to Japan and becoming a favorite of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese people</a>. Keller met every U.S. President from <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> and was friends with many famous figures, including <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>. Keller and Twain were both considered radicals at the beginning of the 20th century, and as a consequence, their political views have been forgotten or glossed over in the popular mind.<sup id="cite_ref-loewen_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loewen-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Keller was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialist Party</a> and actively campaigned and wrote in support of the working class from 1909 to 1921. Many of her speeches and writings were about women's right to vote and the impacts of war. She had speech therapy in order to have her voice heard better by the public. When the Rockefeller-owned press refused to print her articles, she protested until her work was finally published.<sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-30">[30]</a></sup> She supported Socialist Party candidate <a href="/wiki/Eugene_V._Debs" title="Eugene V. Debs">Eugene V. Debs</a> in each of his campaigns for the presidency. Before reading <a href="/wiki/Progress_and_poverty" class="mw-redirect" title="Progress and poverty"><i>Progress and Poverty</i></a>, Helen Keller was already a socialist who believed that <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgism</a> was a good step in the right direction.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> She later wrote of finding "in <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>'s philosophy a rare beauty and power of inspiration, and a splendid faith in the essential nobility of human nature."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Keller claimed that newspaper columnists who had praised her courage and intelligence before she expressed her socialist views now called attention to her disabilities. The editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Eagle" title="Brooklyn Eagle">Brooklyn Eagle</a></i> wrote that her "mistakes sprung out of the manifest limitations of her development." Keller responded to that editor, referring to having met him before he knew of her political views:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>At that time the compliments he paid me were so generous that I blush to remember them. But now that I have come out for socialism he reminds me and the public that I am blind and deaf and especially liable to error. I must have shrunk in intelligence during the years since I met him.&#160;... Oh, ridiculous <i>Brooklyn Eagle</i>! Socially blind and deaf, it defends an intolerable system, a system that is the cause of much of the physical blindness and deafness which we are trying to prevent.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Keller joined the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Workers_of_the_World" title="Industrial Workers of the World">Industrial Workers of the World</a> (the IWW, known as the Wobblies) in 1912,<sup id="cite_ref-loewen_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loewen-34">[34]</a></sup> saying that parliamentary socialism was "sinking in the political bog". She wrote for the IWW between 1916 and 1918. In <i>Why I Became an IWW</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Keller explained that her motivation for activism came in part from her concern about blindness and other disabilities:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I was appointed on a commission to investigate the conditions of the blind. For the first time I, who had thought blindness a misfortune beyond human control, found that too much of it was traceable to wrong industrial conditions, often caused by the selfishness and greed of employers. And the social evil contributed its share. I found that poverty drove women to a life of shame that ended in blindness.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The last sentence refers to prostitution and <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, the former a frequent cause of the latter, and the latter a leading cause of blindness. In the same interview, Keller also cited the <a href="/wiki/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike" class="mw-redirect" title="1912 Lawrence Textile Strike">1912 strike of textile workers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lawrence,_Massachusetts" title="Lawrence, Massachusetts">Lawrence, Massachusetts</a> for instigating her support of socialism.</p>
<p>Like Alexander Graham Bell and others, Keller supported <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>. In 1915 she wrote in favor of refusing life-saving medical procedures to infants with severe mental impairments or physical deformities, stating that their lives were not worthwhile and they would likely become criminals.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Keller also expressed concerns about <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">human overpopulation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmquotes_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmquotes-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wpbquotes_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wpbquotes-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_Keller13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Helen_Keller13.jpg/220px-Helen_Keller13.jpg" width="220" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Helen_Keller13.jpg/330px-Helen_Keller13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Helen_Keller13.jpg/440px-Helen_Keller13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1033" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_Keller13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Helen Keller, circa 1912</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Keller wrote a total of 12 published books and several articles.</p>
<p>One of her earliest pieces of writing, at age 11, was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frost_King" title="The Frost King">The Frost King</a></i> (1891). There were allegations that this story had been <a href="/wiki/Plagiarized" class="mw-redirect" title="Plagiarized">plagiarized</a> from <i>The Frost Fairies</i> by Margaret Canby. An investigation into the matter revealed that Keller may have experienced a case of <a href="/wiki/Cryptomnesia" title="Cryptomnesia">cryptomnesia</a>, which was that she had Canby's story read to her but forgot about it, while the memory remained in her subconscious.<sup id="cite_ref-rnib_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnib-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>At age 22, Keller published her autobiography, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Life_(biography)" title="The Story of My Life (biography)">The Story of My Life</a></i> (1903), with help from Sullivan and Sullivan's husband, John Macy. It recounts the story of her life up to age 21 and was written during her time in college.</p>
<p>Keller wrote <i>The World I Live In</i> in 1908, giving readers an insight into how she felt about the world.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <i>Out of the Dark</i>, a series of essays on socialism, was published in 1913.</p>
<p>When Keller was young, Anne Sullivan introduced her to <a href="/wiki/Phillips_Brooks" title="Phillips Brooks">Phillips Brooks</a>, who introduced her to Christianity, Keller famously saying: "I always knew He was there, but I didn't know His name!"<sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_of_God_1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_of_God_1-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_of_God_2_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_of_God_2-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_of_God_3_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_of_God_3-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> autobiography, <i>My Religion</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-MyReligion_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MyReligion-47">[47]</a></sup> was published in 1927 and then in 1994 extensively revised and re-issued under the title <i><a href="/wiki/Light_in_My_Darkness" title="Light in My Darkness">Light in My Darkness</a></i>. It advocates the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a>, the Christian revelator and theologian who gives a spiritual interpretation of the teachings of the Bible and who claims that the <a href="/wiki/Second_coming" class="mw-redirect" title="Second coming">second coming</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> has already taken place. Adherents use several names to describe themselves, including Second Advent Christian, <a href="/wiki/Swedenborgian" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedenborgian">Swedenborgian</a>, and New Church.</p>
<p>Keller described the progressive views of her belief in these words:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>But in Swedenborg's teaching it [Divine Providence] is shown to be the government of God's Love and Wisdom and the creation of uses. Since His Life cannot be less in one being than another, or His Love manifested less fully in one thing than another, His Providence must needs be universal&#160;... He has provided religion of some kind everywhere, and it does not matter to what race or creed anyone belongs if he is faithful to his ideals of right living.<sup id="cite_ref-MyReligion_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MyReligion-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h2>
<p>Keller suffered a series of strokes in 1961 and spent the last years of her life at her home.<sup id="cite_ref-rnib_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnib-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 14, 1964, <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> awarded her the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>, one of the United States' two highest civilian honors. In 1965 she was elected to the <a href="/wiki/National_Women%27s_Hall_of_Fame" title="National Women's Hall of Fame">National Women's Hall of Fame</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1964_New_York_World%27s_Fair" title="1964 New York World's Fair">New York World's Fair</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rnib_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnib-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Keller devoted much of her later life to raising funds for the <a href="/wiki/American_Foundation_for_the_Blind" title="American Foundation for the Blind">American Foundation for the Blind</a>. She died in her sleep on June 1, 1968, at her home, Arcan Ridge, located in <a href="/wiki/Easton,_Connecticut" title="Easton, Connecticut">Easton, Connecticut</a>, a few weeks short of her eighty-eighth birthday. A service was held in her honor at the <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">National Cathedral</a> in Washington, D.C., her body was cremated and her ashes were placed there next to her constant companions, Anne Sullivan and Polly Thomson. She was buried at the Washington National Cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Portrayals">Portrayals</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury,_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury%2C_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG/220px-Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury%2C_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury%2C_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG/330px-Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury%2C_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury%2C_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG/440px-Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury%2C_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_Sullivan_-_Helen_Keller_memorial_-_Tewksbury,_Massachusetts_-_DSC00072.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"<a href="/wiki/Anne_Sullivan" title="Anne Sullivan">Anne Sullivan</a>&#160;– Helen Keller Memorial"—a bronze sculpture in <a href="/wiki/Tewksbury,_Massachusetts" title="Tewksbury, Massachusetts">Tewksbury, Massachusetts</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Keller's life has been interpreted many times. She appeared in a <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Deliverance_(1919_movie)" class="mw-redirect" title="Deliverance (1919 movie)">Deliverance</a></i> (1919), which told her story in a melodramatic, allegorical style.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>She was also the subject of the documentaries <i><a href="/wiki/Helen_Keller_in_Her_Story" title="Helen Keller in Her Story">Helen Keller in Her Story</a></i>, narrated by <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Cornell" title="Katharine Cornell">Katharine Cornell</a>, and <i>The Story of Helen Keller</i>, part of the Famous Americans series produced by <a href="/wiki/Hearst_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Hearst Corporation">Hearst Entertainment</a>.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_Worker" title="The Miracle Worker">The Miracle Worker</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Literature_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Literature cycle">cycle</a> of dramatic works ultimately derived from her autobiography, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Life_(biography)" title="The Story of My Life (biography)">The Story of My Life</a></i>. The various dramas each describe the relationship between Keller and Sullivan, depicting how the teacher led her from a state of almost <a href="/wiki/Feral_children" class="mw-redirect" title="Feral children">feral wildness</a> into education, activism, and intellectual celebrity. The common title of the cycle echoes <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>'s description of Sullivan as a "miracle worker." Its first realization was the 1957 <i><a href="/wiki/Playhouse_90" title="Playhouse 90">Playhouse 90</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Screenplay" title="Screenplay">teleplay</a> of that title by <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson_(playwright)" title="William Gibson (playwright)">William Gibson</a>. He adapted it for a <a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_Worker_(play)" title="The Miracle Worker (play)">Broadway production in 1959</a> and an Oscar-winning <a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_Worker_(1962_film)" title="The Miracle Worker (1962 film)">feature film in 1962</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bancroft" title="Anne Bancroft">Anne Bancroft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patty_Duke" title="Patty Duke">Patty Duke</a>. It was remade for television in 1979 and 2000.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg/220px-Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg/330px-Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="338" data-file-height="450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helen_keller_patty_duke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
With <a href="/wiki/Patty_Duke" title="Patty Duke">Patty Duke</a>, who portrayed Helen Keller in both the play and film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_Worker" title="The Miracle Worker">The Miracle Worker</a></i> (1962). In a 1979 remake, Patty Duke played <a href="/wiki/Anne_Sullivan" title="Anne Sullivan">Anne Sullivan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1984, Keller's life story was made into a TV movie called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Miracle_Continues" title="The Miracle Continues">The Miracle Continues</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This film that entailed the semi-sequel to <i>The Miracle Worker</i> recounts her college years and her early adult life. None of the early movies hint at the <a href="/wiki/Social_activism" class="mw-redirect" title="Social activism">social activism</a> that would become the hallmark of Keller's later life, although a <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a> version produced in 2000 states in the credits that she became an activist for <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">social equality</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> movie <i><a href="/wiki/Black_(2005_film)" title="Black (2005 film)">Black</a></i> (2005) was largely based on Keller's story, from her childhood to her graduation.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>A documentary called <i>Shining Soul: Helen Keller's Spiritual Life and Legacy</i> was produced by the Swedenborg Foundation in the same year. The film focuses on the role played by <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a>'s spiritual theology in her life and how it inspired Keller's triumph over her triple disabilities of blindness, deafness and a severe speech impediment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>On March 6, 2008, the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Historic_Genealogical_Society" title="New England Historic Genealogical Society">New England Historic Genealogical Society</a> announced that a staff member had discovered a rare 1888 photograph showing Helen and Anne, which, although previously published, had escaped widespread attention.<sup id="cite_ref-independent_picture_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent_picture-52">[52]</a></sup> Depicting Helen holding one of her many dolls, it is believed to be the earliest surviving photograph of Anne Sullivan Macy.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Video footage showing Helen Keller learning to mimic speech sounds also exists.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>A biography of Helen Keller was written by the German Jewish author <a href="/wiki/H.J.Kaeser" class="mw-redirect" title="H.J.Kaeser">H.J.Kaeser</a>.</p>
<p>A 10-by-7-foot painting titled <i>The Advocate: Tribute to Helen Keller</i> was created by three artists from Kerala as a tribute to Helen Keller. The Painting was created in association with a non-profit organization Art d'Hope Foundation, artists groups Palette People and XakBoX Design &amp; Art Studio.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> This painting was created for a fundraising event to help blind students in India <sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and was inaugurated by M. G. Rajamanikyam, IAS (District Collector Ernakulam) on Helen Keller day (June 27, 2016).<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The painting depicts the major events of Helen Keller's life and is one of the biggest paintings done based on Helen Keller's life.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_honors">Posthumous honors</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alabama_quarter,_reverse_side,_2003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Alabama_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2003.jpg/220px-Alabama_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2003.jpg" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Alabama_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2003.jpg/330px-Alabama_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Alabama_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2003.jpg/440px-Alabama_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1154" data-file-height="1147" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alabama_quarter,_reverse_side,_2003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Helen Keller as depicted on the Alabama state quarter</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A preschool for the deaf and hard of hearing in <a href="/wiki/Mysore" title="Mysore">Mysore</a>, India, was originally named after Helen Keller by its founder, <a href="/wiki/K._K._Srinivasan" title="K. K. Srinivasan">K. K. Srinivasan</a>. In 1999, Keller was listed in <a href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_List_of_Widely_Admired_People" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallup's List of Widely Admired People">Gallup's Most Widely Admired People of the 20th century</a>.</p>
<p>In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> honored its native daughter on its <a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" title="50 State Quarters">state quarter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The Alabama state quarter is the only circulating US coin to feature braille.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Helen Keller Hospital in <a href="/wiki/Sheffield,_Alabama" title="Sheffield, Alabama">Sheffield, Alabama</a>, is dedicated to her.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Streets are named after Helen Keller in <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>, Switzerland, in the USA, in <a href="/wiki/Getafe" title="Getafe">Getafe</a>, Spain, in <a href="/wiki/Lod" title="Lod">Lod</a>, Israel,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>, Portugal<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a>, France.</p>
<p>A stamp was issued in 1980 by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> depicting Keller and Sullivan, to mark the centennial of Keller's birth.</p>
<p>On October 7, 2009, a bronze statue of Helen Keller was added to the <a href="/wiki/National_Statuary_Hall_Collection" title="National Statuary Hall Collection">National Statuary Hall Collection</a>, as a replacement for the State of Alabama's former 1908 statue of the education reformer <a href="/wiki/Jabez_Lamar_Monroe_Curry" title="Jabez Lamar Monroe Curry">Jabez Lamar Monroe Curry</a>. It is displayed in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol_Visitor_Center" title="United States Capitol Visitor Center">United States Capitol Visitor Center</a> and depicts Keller as a seven-year-old child standing at a <a href="/wiki/Hand_pump" title="Hand pump">water pump</a>. The statue represents the seminal moment in Keller's life when she understood her first word: W-A-T-E-R, as signed into her hand by teacher Anne Sullivan. The pedestal base bears a quotation in raised Latin and braille letters: "The best and most beautiful things in the world cannot be seen or even touched, they must be felt with the heart."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The statue is the first one of a person with a disability and of a child to be permanently displayed at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">U.S. Capitol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archival_material">Archival material</span></h2>
<p>Archival material of Helen Keller stored in New York was lost when the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">Twin Towers</a> were destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Helen Keller Archives are owned by the <a href="/wiki/American_Foundation_for_the_Blind" title="American Foundation for the Blind">American Foundation for the Blind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-Knowledge_of_God_2-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Knowledge_of_God_2_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Helms, Harold E. (April 30, 2004). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=PzDfvkvqI0IC&amp;pg=PA78&amp;dq=Helen+Keller+but+I+didn't+know+His+name+Helen+Keller#v=onepage&amp;q=Helen%20Keller%20but%20I%20didn't%20know%20His%20name%20Helen%20Keller&amp;f=false"><i>God's Final Answer</i></a>. Xulon Press. p.&#160;78. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-59467-410-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-59467-410-5">978-1-59467-410-5</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">March 15,</span> 2016</span>. <q>A favorite story about Helen Keller concerns her first introduction to the gospel. When Helen, who was both blind and deaf, learned to communicate, Anne Sullivan, her teacher, decided that it was time for her to hear about Jesus Christ. Anne called for Phillips Brooks, the most famous preacher in Boston. With Sullivan interpreting for him, he talked to Helen Keller about Christ. It wasn't long until a smile lighted up her face. Through her teacher she said, "Mr. Brooks, I have always known about God, but until now I didn't know His name."</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHelen+Keller&amp;rft.aufirst=Harold+E.&amp;rft.aulast=Helms&amp;rft.btitle=God%27s+Final+Answer&amp;rft.date=2004-04-30&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-59467-410-5&amp;rft.pages=78&amp;rft.pub=Xulon+Press&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DPzDfvkvqI0IC%26pg%3DPA78%26dq%3DHelen%2BKeller%2Bbut%2BI%2Bdidn%27t%2Bknow%2BHis%2Bname%2BHelen%2BKeller%23v%3Donepage%26q%3DHelen%2520Keller%2520but%2520I%2520didn%27t%2520know%2520His%2520name%2520Helen%2520Keller%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
